\section{K1A3: Actual Extraction of \texorpdfstring{$\kappa_*$}{kappa*} and the Competing-Channel Structure}

\section{1. Goal}

At the previous step, the one-protected-mode theorem was reduced to the hypotheses:
\[
\mu_\infty>0,\qquad a_0>0,\qquad b_0>0,
\]
together with the existence of a unique signed lowest projected channel
\[
\kappa_*<0
\]
and the exclusion of all competing projected channels.

The present K1A3 task is to extract this \(\kappa_*\) and the competing-channel structure from the actual angular decomposition of the single-defect operator.

\section{2. Angular reduction and raw \texorpdfstring{$S^2$}{S²} spectrum}

After radial reduction, the angular dependence is governed by the spherical Dirac operator
\[
\mathcal D_{S^2}.
\]
Its spectrum is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal D_{S^2}Y_{\kappa m\varsigma}=\kappa\,Y_{\kappa m\varsigma},
\qquad
\kappa\in\{\pm1,\pm2,\pm3,\dots\}.
}
\label{eq:kappa_raw_spectrum}
\end{equation}

Therefore the raw lowest angular magnitude is always
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\kappa|_{\min}=1.
}
\label{eq:kappa_lowest_abs}
\end{equation}

So, if after remote-gap isolation and low-slot projection there exists a unique signed lowest surviving channel, then it must be one of
\[
\kappa_*=-1
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\kappa_*=+1.
\]

This is already enough to reduce the abstract “unique signed lowest channel” hypothesis to a two-sign choice.

\section{3. Sign selection from the core superpotential}

The protected-channel counting is controlled by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
W_\kappa^{\rm core}(r)=\mu(r)+\frac{\kappa\,b(r)}{r}.
}
\label{eq:Wcore_recall}
\end{equation}

Near the core,
\[
W_\kappa^{\rm core}(r)=\mu_0+\frac{\kappa b_0}{r}+O(r).
\]
So the sign of the singular \(1/r\) term is the sign of \(\kappa b_0\).

Assume, as in the working theorem, that
\[
b_0>0.
\]
Then:
\begin{itemize}
\item for \(\kappa<0\), the singular term is negative;
\item for \(\kappa>0\), the singular term is positive.
\end{itemize}

But under \(\mu_\infty>0\), the asymptotically decaying branch is the \(+\)-chiral branch.
So the only way to get a protected channel is that the same \(u_{\kappa,+}\) branch be:
\begin{enumerate}
\item decaying at infinity;
\item and \(L^2\)-regular at the core.
\end{enumerate}

The negative singular core sign is exactly what makes this possible in the sufficient theorem.
Therefore, once \(b_0>0\), the protected lowest signed channel must be negative:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_*<0.
}
\label{eq:kappa_negative_selected}
\end{equation}

Since the lowest possible \(|\kappa|\) is \(1\), it follows that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_*=-1.
}
\label{eq:kappa_star_minus_one}
\end{equation}

This is the actual sign extraction hidden inside the earlier profile theorem.

\section{4. Competing-channel structure}

Because the raw angular spectrum is
\[
\kappa=\pm1,\pm2,\pm3,\dots,
\]
and the unique lowest signed protected channel is \(\kappa_*=-1\), every competing projected channel must satisfy one of the following:
\begin{enumerate}
\item it is removed by the remote-gap theorem;
\item or it survives but has positive sign, hence
\[
\kappa\ge 1.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Thus the projected competing structure becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1.
}
\label{eq:kappa_comp_ge_1}
\end{equation}

This is exactly the structural content needed in the previous one-mode theorem.

\section{5. Explicit canonical branch interpretation}

For the explicit canonical branch
\[
T_\star=T^1,\qquad \gamma_E=1,\qquad \gamma_O=0,
\]
the practical lowest-channel statement already recorded in the notes is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_*=-1,\qquad \kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1.
}
\label{eq:canonical_branch_kappa}
\end{equation}

So K1A3 is not inventing a new condition.
It is identifying the precise operator-theoretic meaning of the branch statement already used operationally.

\section{6. Reduction of the decisive inequality}

The previous transport bridge was
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
2|\kappa_*|\,\xi_E g_E>\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c.
}
\label{eq:bridge_general}
\end{equation}

Now insert the actual lowest-channel extraction
\[
|\kappa_*|=1.
\]
Then \eqref{eq:bridge_general} becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\xi_E g_E>\xi_c g_c.
}
\label{eq:bridge_lowest_channel}
\end{equation}

Likewise, for every competing projected channel \(\kappa\ge 1\), the exclusion condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
2\kappa\,\xi_E g_E>\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c.
}
\label{eq:bridge_competing_channels}
\end{equation}

So after K1A3, the entire lowest-channel classification is encoded in the explicit core coefficients and this finite scalar bridge.

\section{7. Actual content of K1A3}

Therefore K1A3 consists of exactly three operator-theoretic facts:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the projected angular spectrum is inherited from the spherical Dirac spectrum, so
\[
|\kappa|_{\min}=1;
\]

\item the protected-channel sign is fixed by the sign of \(\kappa b_0\), and for \(b_0>0\) this selects \(\kappa_*=-1\);

\item every surviving competitor either is removed by remote-gap isolation or satisfies \(\kappa\ge 1\).
\end{enumerate}

Thus the abstract hypothesis “unique signed lowest channel” has now been sharpened to the explicit working statement
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_*=-1,
\qquad
\kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1.
}
\label{eq:K1A3_actual_output}
\end{equation}

\section{8. Honest endpoint}

What remains after K1A3 is no longer the identity of \(\kappa_*\).
It is the explicit verification that the projected operator on the intended branch indeed realizes:
\[
\mu_\infty>0,\qquad
a_0>0,\qquad
b_0>0,
\qquad
\kappa_*=-1,
\qquad
\kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1,
\]
together with the remote-gap exclusion of all higher sectors.

So K1A3 closes the channel-label part of the problem.
The remaining work is the actual branchwise inequality verification.

\section{Final Summary}

The projected angular channel labels come from the spherical Dirac spectrum
\[
\kappa\in\{\pm1,\pm2,\pm3,\dots\},
\]
so the raw lowest angular magnitude is always
\[
|\kappa|_{\min}=1.
\]

Therefore, if after remote-gap isolation and low-slot projection there exists a unique signed lowest surviving channel, it must be either \(\kappa_*=-1\) or \(\kappa_*=+1\).

The protected-channel sign is then determined by the core superpotential
\[
W_\kappa^{\rm core}(r)=\mu(r)+\frac{\kappa b(r)}{r}.
\]
When \(b_0>0\), the singular core term has negative sign precisely for \(\kappa<0\).
Under \(\mu_\infty>0\), this is the sign needed for the asymptotically decaying chiral generator to remain \(L^2\)-regular at the core.

Hence the protected lowest signed channel is
\[
\boxed{
\kappa_*=-1.
}
\]

All competing projected channels are either removed by remote-gap isolation or satisfy
\[
\boxed{
\kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1.
}
\]

This is exactly the practical lowest-channel structure already used in the explicit canonical branch.

Consequently, the decisive transport bridge
\[
2|\kappa_*|\,\xi_E g_E>\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c
\]
reduces to
\[
\boxed{
\xi_E g_E>\xi_c g_c
}
\]
in the lowest-channel case.

Thus K1A3 closes the channel-label part of the one-mode problem:
the remaining work is only the explicit branchwise verification of the core/bulk inequalities and remote-gap exclusion.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{K1A4: Practical One-Defect One-Mode Pass Theorem on the Explicit Canonical Working Branch}

\section{1. Reused inputs from K1A2 and K1A3}

From the previous K1A steps, we reuse:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_*=-1,
\qquad
\kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_kappa}
\end{equation}
and the radial core coefficients
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
a_0=\frac{\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c}{z_{0,0}},
\qquad
b_0=\frac{\xi_E g_E}{z_{0,0}},
\qquad
c_0=\frac{\xi_O g_O}{z_{0,0}}.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_a0b0c0}
\end{equation}

The bulk mass parameter is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mu_\infty
=
\frac{
m(\sigma_\infty)-3\chi_\infty u_X(\sigma_\infty)
}{
z_0(\sigma_\infty)
}.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_muinf}
\end{equation}

The general practical one-defect / one-mode criterion is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{full}}>\eta_{\mathrm{rem},\rho},
\qquad
\kappa_*<0\ \text{ unique},
\qquad
\mu_\infty>0,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_general1}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c>0,
\qquad
\xi_E g_E>0,
\qquad
2|\kappa_*|\,\xi_E g_E>\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_general2}
\end{equation}
with every competitor satisfying
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
2\kappa\,\xi_E g_E>\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_general3}
\end{equation}


\section{2. Explicit canonical branch insertion}

Now specialize to the explicit canonical branch
\[
\boxed{
T_\star=T^1,\qquad \gamma_E=1,\qquad \gamma_O=0.
}
\]
Then the Bloch/Class-II coefficients are already explicit:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_c
=
\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_gc}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_E
=
\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,
\frac{\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,
\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
g_O=0.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_gE}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_0=0,
\qquad
b_0
=
\frac{\xi_E}{z_{0,0}}
\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{\beta_X}{M_X^2}\theta_0\kappa,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_b0}
\end{equation}
and defining
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_c:=\frac{\xi_c g_c}{\xi_E g_E}
=
\frac{\xi_c}{\xi_E}\frac{3}{32}\alpha_X(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_Rc}
\end{equation}
one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
a_0=b_0(1+R_c).
}
\label{eq:K1A4_a0}
\end{equation}
:contentReference[oaicite:10]{index=10}

\section{3. Collapse of the lowest-channel inequalities}

Since \(\kappa_*=-1\), one has \(|\kappa_*|=1\).
Therefore the decisive bridge inequality
\[
2|\kappa_*|\,\xi_E g_E>\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c
\]
reduces to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\xi_E g_E>\xi_c g_c.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_bridge_lowest}
\end{equation}

In terms of \(R_c\), the three low-core inequalities become:
\[
\xi_E g_E>0
\iff
b_0>0,
\]
\[
\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c>0
\iff
a_0>0
\iff
1+R_c>0,
\]
and
\[
\xi_E g_E>\xi_c g_c
\iff
R_c<1.
\]
Hence the entire low-core part is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
-1<R_c<1,
\qquad
\alpha>0.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_lowcore_reduced}
\end{equation}

Thus the practical lowest-channel one-mode criterion becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
-1<R_c<1,
\qquad
\alpha>0,
\qquad
\mu_\infty>0,
\qquad
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{full}}>\eta_{\mathrm{rem},\rho}.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_fourchecks}
\end{equation}
This is exactly the explicit canonical branch reduction already recorded in the notes. 

\section{4. Primitive witness insertion for the longitudinal channel}

The same explicit canonical witness branch also gives the more microscopic longitudinal coefficient:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,
\Gamma_{JJ}\,
m_\parallel\,u_\parallel(T^1)\,
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_lambdaJJ}
\end{equation}
while the transverse coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,
\beta_X\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta=0.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_alpha_beta}
\end{equation}

Therefore a practical sufficient witness condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma_{JJ}\neq0,\qquad
\beta_X\neq0,\qquad
m_\parallel\neq0,\qquad
u_\parallel(T^1)\neq0,\qquad
\theta_0\kappa\neq0.
}
\label{eq:K1A4_witness_nonzero}
\end{equation}
Under \eqref{eq:K1A4_witness_nonzero},
\[
\lambda_\parallel\neq0,
\qquad
\alpha\neq0,
\qquad
\beta=0,
\]
so the branch is a nondegenerate carrier witness branch already at the explicit canonical level. :contentReference[oaicite:12]{index=12}

\section{5. Practical K1A4 theorem}

Combining the low-core reduction \eqref{eq:K1A4_fourchecks} with the explicit witness insertion \eqref{eq:K1A4_witness_nonzero}, we obtain:

\begin{theorem}[Practical one-defect one-mode pass theorem on the explicit canonical working branch]
Assume the same explicit canonical working branch
\[
T_\star=T^1,\qquad \gamma_E=1,\qquad \gamma_O=0,
\]
and the practical lowest-channel structure
\[
\kappa_*=-1,\qquad \kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1.
\]
If, in addition,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma_{JJ}\neq0,\qquad
\beta_X\neq0,\qquad
m_\parallel\neq0,\qquad
u_\parallel(T^1)\neq0,\qquad
\theta_0\kappa\neq0,
}
\label{eq:K1A4_thm1}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
-1<R_c<1,
\qquad
\alpha>0,
\qquad
\mu_\infty>0,
\qquad
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{full}}>\eta_{\mathrm{rem},\rho},
}
\label{eq:K1A4_thm2}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb H_{\rm C})=1,
}
\end{equation}
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{one defect } \leftrightarrow \text{ one protected light mode.}
}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Condition \eqref{eq:K1A4_thm1} implies
\[
\lambda_\parallel\neq0,\qquad \alpha\neq0,\qquad \beta=0,
\]
so the explicit canonical branch realizes both a longitudinal witness and a transverse witness.

Condition \eqref{eq:K1A4_thm2} is exactly the reduced practical lowest-channel one-mode criterion on that branch.

By the previously established working one-defect/one-mode theorem, these imply
\[
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb H_{\rm C})=1,
\]
hence one defect carries exactly one protected light mode.
\end{proof}

\section{6. Honest scope}

This is the strongest honest K1A4 result on the present explicit canonical working branch.

It is already enough for a practical theorem-level completion of the single-defect one-mode statement on that branch.

However, it is still not the fully unconditional final microscopic TECT theorem, because the notes explicitly keep open the actual branch evaluation of:
\[
\xi_E,\qquad \xi_c,\qquad \mu_\infty,\qquad
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{full}}-\eta_{\mathrm{rem},\rho},
\]
as well as the final overlap calibrations in the complete theory.

\section{Final Summary}

At K1A4, the one-defect one-mode problem is no longer structural.
On the explicit canonical working branch
\[
T_\star=T^1,\qquad \gamma_E=1,\qquad \gamma_O=0,
\]
the coefficients are already explicit:
\[
g_c
=
\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
\qquad
g_E
=
\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,
\frac{\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,
\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
g_O=0.
\]

With
\[
a_0=\frac{\xi_E g_E+\xi_c g_c}{z_{0,0}},
\qquad
b_0=\frac{\xi_E g_E}{z_{0,0}},
\qquad
R_c=\frac{\xi_c}{\xi_E}\frac{3}{32}\alpha_X(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\]
and in the practical lowest-channel case
\[
\kappa_*=-1,\qquad \kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1,
\]
the low-core one-mode inequalities reduce exactly to
\[
\boxed{
-1<R_c<1,
\qquad
\alpha>0,
\qquad
\mu_\infty>0,
\qquad
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{full}}>\eta_{\mathrm{rem},\rho}.
}
\]

The primitive witness layer is also explicit:
\[
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,
\Gamma_{JJ}\,
m_\parallel\,u_\parallel(T^1)\,
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
\]
\[
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\beta_X\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta=0.
\]
So a practical sufficient witness condition is
\[
\Gamma_{JJ}\neq0,\qquad
\beta_X\neq0,\qquad
m_\parallel\neq0,\qquad
u_\parallel(T^1)\neq0,\qquad
\theta_0\kappa\neq0.
\]

Therefore, under these finite scalar checks,
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb H_{\rm C})=1,
}
\]
and hence
\[
\boxed{
\text{one defect } \leftrightarrow \text{ one protected light mode.}
}
\]

This closes K1A4 at practical theorem level on the explicit canonical working branch, while the fully unconditional final microscopic theorem still depends on the final finite audits of the intended branch data.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{K1B: Spectral Isolation of the Three-Defect Family Subspace}

\section{1. Input from K1A}

Assume K1A has already been established at practical theorem level on the chosen working branch.
That is, a single isolated defect supports exactly one exponentially localized protected light mode
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}\psi_\star=\lambda_\star\psi_\star,
\qquad
\|\psi_\star(x)\|\lesssim e^{-\mu |x|},
\qquad
\dim\ker_{\rm low}(\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}-\lambda_\star)=1.
}
\label{eq:K1B_input_single}
\end{equation}

The rank-one single-defect case is exactly the natural family-space input:
if one defect carried \(q>1\) protected low modes, then three defects would generate \(3q\) light states rather than \(3\). :contentReference[oaicite:5]{index=5}

\section{2. Three translated defect modes}

Let \(X_1,X_2,X_3\in\mathbb R^3\) be three sufficiently separated defect centers, and let the three-defect background be a weakly interacting superposition of three isolated defects.

Define the translated approximate localized modes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\psi_a(x):=\psi_\star(x-X_a),
\qquad
a=1,2,3.
}
\label{eq:K1B_translated}
\end{equation}
If a microscopic connection is present, one should use parallel-transported translates
\[
\psi_a(x)=U_a(x)\psi_\star(x-X_a),
\]
but this does not change the algebra below. :contentReference[oaicite:6]{index=6}

Let
\[
R_{ab}:=|X_a-X_b|,
\qquad
R_{\min}:=\min_{a\neq b}R_{ab}.
\]

Because \(\psi_\star\) is exponentially localized,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle
=
\delta_{ab}
+
O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}}).
}
\label{eq:K1B_overlap}
\end{equation}

Hence the Gram matrix
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G_{ab}:=\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle
}
\end{equation}
satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G=I+O(e^{-\mu R_{\min}}).
}
\label{eq:K1B_Gram}
\end{equation}
Therefore, for sufficiently large separation,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G \text{ is invertible.}
}
\label{eq:K1B_Gram_invertible}
\end{equation}
So the approximate low subspace
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal S_{\rm app}:=\operatorname{span}\{\psi_1,\psi_2,\psi_3\}
}
\label{eq:K1B_Sapp}
\end{equation}
is genuinely three-dimensional. :contentReference[oaicite:7]{index=7}

\section{3. Projected approximate light matrix}

Let
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}
\]
be the microscopic reference operator on the three-defect background.

Projecting onto the approximate low subspace gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_{ab}^{\rm app}:=\langle \psi_a,\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\psi_b\rangle.
}
\label{eq:K1B_Mapp}
\end{equation}

Because each \(\psi_a\) is localized near the \(a\)-th defect and solves the isolated one-defect problem up to exponentially small interaction corrections, one has
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_{ab}^{\rm app}
=
\lambda_\star G_{ab}+T_{ab},
\qquad
T_{ab}=O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}}).
}
\label{eq:K1B_tightbinding}
\end{equation}

Thus the low three-defect cluster is controlled by a finite \(3\times3\) tight-binding-type matrix with exponentially small off-diagonal couplings. 

\section{4. Orthonormalized localized basis}

Since \(G\) is invertible, define the orthonormalized localized basis
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\phi_a:=\sum_{b=1}^3 \psi_b\,(G^{-1/2})_{ba}.
}
\label{eq:K1B_phi}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\langle \phi_a,\phi_b\rangle=\delta_{ab}.
}
\label{eq:K1B_orthonormal}
\end{equation}

The projected approximate light matrix becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L^{\rm app}:=G^{-1/2}M^{\rm app}G^{-1/2},
\qquad
L^{\rm app}_{ab}
=
\langle \phi_a,\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\phi_b\rangle.
}
\label{eq:K1B_Lapp}
\end{equation}

So already at the approximate level, the three-defect low sector is a finite Hermitian \(3\times3\) matrix problem. This is exactly the K1B precursor to later family Hamiltonians. :contentReference[oaicite:9]{index=9}

\section{5. Heavy-gap hypothesis and exact low projector}

Now let the rest of the spectrum of \(\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\) stay at least a distance \(\Delta_H>0\) away from \(\lambda_\star\) in the isolated-defect limit.

Assume the interaction/tunneling scale obeys
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|T\|+\|G-I\|\,|\lambda_\star|
<
\frac12 \Delta_H.
}
\label{eq:K1B_gap_condition}
\end{equation}

Then the three approximate low eigenvalues obtained from \(L^{\rm app}\) remain separated from the heavy spectrum.
Therefore there exists a contour \(\Gamma\) enclosing exactly those three low eigenvalues and no other spectrum.

Define the exact Riesz projector
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P_L
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\oint_\Gamma
(z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}\,dz.
}
\label{eq:K1B_PL}
\end{equation}
Then the exact light family space is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L:=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L).
}
\label{eq:K1B_HL}
\end{equation}

Since \(\Gamma\) encloses exactly three isolated eigenvalues, one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\dim \mathcal H_L=3.
}
\label{eq:K1B_rank3}
\end{equation}

This is precisely the K1B statement that a three-dimensional family subspace is isolated. :contentReference[oaicite:10]{index=10}

\section{6. Practical theorem-level K1B statement}

We can now state the practical K1B theorem on the current route.

\begin{theorem}[Practical K1B triplet-isolation theorem]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item K1A holds at practical theorem level, i.e. one isolated defect carries exactly one exponentially localized protected light mode \(\psi_\star\);
\item three defect centers \(X_1,X_2,X_3\) are sufficiently well separated;
\item the three-defect background is a weakly interacting superposition of the three isolated defects;
\item the heavy spectrum remains separated by a gap \(\Delta_H\) satisfying \eqref{eq:K1B_gap_condition}.
\end{enumerate}
Then:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\dim \mathcal S_{\rm app}=3,
\qquad
\operatorname{rank}P_L=3,
\qquad
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)
}
\label{eq:K1B_practical_conclusion}
\end{equation}
and hence a three-dimensional common light family subspace is isolated.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By exponential localization, the overlaps obey \eqref{eq:K1B_overlap}, so the Gram matrix satisfies \eqref{eq:K1B_Gram} and is invertible for sufficiently large separation. Therefore \(\dim \mathcal S_{\rm app}=3\).

Projecting \(\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\) onto \(\mathcal S_{\rm app}\) gives the finite matrix \(M^{\rm app}\) with exponentially small tunneling correction \(T\). Under the gap condition \eqref{eq:K1B_gap_condition}, the three approximate low eigenvalues remain isolated from the heavy spectrum. Hence a contour \(\Gamma\) exists that encloses exactly those three eigenvalues, and the corresponding Riesz projector \(P_L\) has rank three.

Therefore the exact isolated family space \(\mathcal H_L\) is three-dimensional.
\end{proof}

\section{7. Honest status}

This closes K1B at practical theorem level, but not yet as a first-principles TECT theorem.

The reason is exactly the same as before:
the single-defect one-mode statement itself is still conditional on the actual TECT defect operator satisfying the Callias-type hypotheses A1--A4, including asymptotic gap and index one. Only after that is verified does the implication
\[
\text{one defect } \leftrightarrow \text{ one light mode}
\Longrightarrow
\operatorname{rank}P_L=3
\]
become a first-principles theorem inside TECT. 

So the correct K1B wording is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{K1B is closed conditionally at practical theorem level,}
}
\end{equation}
namely conditional on K1A and on a sufficiently large separation / heavy-gap regime.

\section{Final Summary}

K1B asks for an isolated three-dimensional family subspace.

Assuming K1A has already supplied one exponentially localized protected light mode per defect,
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}\psi_\star=\lambda_\star\psi_\star,
\qquad
\|\psi_\star(x)\|\lesssim e^{-\mu |x|},
\]
define the translated modes
\[
\psi_a(x)=\psi_\star(x-X_a),
\qquad
a=1,2,3.
\]

For sufficiently well-separated defect centers,
\[
\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle
=
\delta_{ab}+O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}}),
\]
so the Gram matrix
\[
G_{ab}=\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle
\]
satisfies
\[
G=I+O(e^{-\mu R_{\min}})
\]
and is invertible.
Therefore the approximate low subspace
\[
\mathcal S_{\rm app}=\operatorname{span}\{\psi_1,\psi_2,\psi_3\}
\]
is three-dimensional.

Projecting the three-defect operator gives
\[
M_{ab}^{\rm app}
=
\langle \psi_a,\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\psi_b\rangle
=
\lambda_\star G_{ab}+T_{ab},
\qquad
T_{ab}=O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}}).
\]
After orthonormalization, the approximate light matrix is
\[
L^{\rm app}=G^{-1/2}M^{\rm app}G^{-1/2}.
\]

If the heavy spectrum stays separated by a gap \(\Delta_H\) larger than the tunneling/Gram-correction scale, then the exact Riesz projector
\[
P_L
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma (z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}\,dz
\]
is well-defined and satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{rank}P_L=3.
}
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)
}
\]
is the isolated three-dimensional common family subspace.

So K1B is closed at practical theorem level, conditional on K1A and on sufficiently large defect separation with a surviving heavy gap.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{K1C: Effective Preservation of the Isolated Family Space under Sector Perturbations}

\section{1. Input from K1B}

Assume K1B has already been established at practical theorem level.
Thus there exists an isolated three-dimensional low spectral subspace
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L),
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_L=3,
}
\label{eq:K1C_input_HL}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P_L
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma
(z-\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def})^{-1}\,dz
}
\label{eq:K1C_PL}
\end{equation}
projects onto the isolated low cluster of the reference three-defect operator
\[
\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}.
\]

Let
\[
Q_H:=1-P_L
\]
be the heavy-sector projector.
Assume the reference low energy is centered near \(E_*\), and the heavy spectrum obeys
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}\!\bigl(E_*,\operatorname{spec}(Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H)\bigr)\ge \Delta_H>0.
}
\label{eq:K1C_gap}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Sector perturbation and exact effective operator}

For each fermion sector \(f\), define the microscopic perturbed operator
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb O_f
=
\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}
+
\delta\mathbb O_f.
}
\label{eq:K1C_Of}
\end{equation}

Write it in the \(P_L\oplus Q_H\) block form:
\[
\mathbb O_f=
\begin{pmatrix}
L_f & K_f\\
K_f^\dagger & H_f
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
L_f=P_L\mathbb O_fP_L,
\qquad
K_f=P_L\mathbb O_fQ_H,
\qquad
H_f=Q_H\mathbb O_fQ_H.
\]

Then the exact Feshbach/Schur-complement operator on \(\mathcal H_L\) is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f(E)
=
L_f
+
K_f\,(E-H_f)^{-1}K_f^\dagger.
}
\label{eq:K1C_exact_feshbach}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, subtracting the universal reference block and expanding around the unperturbed energy \(E_*\), the exact reduced family operator has the standard form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
-
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,Q_H
\bigl(Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q_H\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
+
O(\delta\mathbb O_f^3).
}
\label{eq:K1C_SW}
\end{equation}

This is exactly the finite family operator already identified in the notes. 

\section{3. Meaning of “effective preservation”}

K1C does not require the family subspace to remain literally invariant under the full perturbed operator in the strong sense
\[
\delta\mathbb O_f \mathcal H_L\subset \mathcal H_L.
\]
That would be too strong.

Instead, the correct notion is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the low sector remains spectrally isolated and the full dynamics near }E_*
\text{ is exactly reducible to a closed operator on }\mathcal H_L.
}
\label{eq:K1C_effective_preservation}
\end{equation}

Thus “effective preservation” means:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the heavy resolvent exists near the low energy,
\item the low-heavy mixing is perturbative,
\item the exact low dynamics is captured by the finite operator \(\mathcal Y_f(E)\) on \(\mathcal H_L\).
\end{enumerate}

\section{4. Practical sufficient condition}

A sufficient practical condition is the finite heavy-gap / mixing inequality
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|\delta\mathbb O_f\|<\frac{\Delta_H}{2},
\qquad
\|K_f\|<\frac{\Delta_H}{2}.
}
\label{eq:K1C_smallness}
\end{equation}

Then for every \(E\) with \(|E-E_*|<\Delta_H/4\), one has
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|(E-H_f)^{-1}\|
\le
\frac{2}{\Delta_H}.
}
\label{eq:K1C_resolvent_bound}
\end{equation}

Consequently, the heavy-mediated correction is bounded by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\|K_f(E-H_f)^{-1}K_f^\dagger\|
\le
\frac{2}{\Delta_H}\|K_f\|^2.
}
\label{eq:K1C_Schur_bound}
\end{equation}

So the sector perturbation does not destroy the family space:
it only renormalizes the finite \(3\times 3\) family operator by a controlled heavy-mediated correction.

\section{5. Hermiticity of the reduced operator}

Assume \(\mathbb O_f\) is self-adjoint.
Then for real \(E\) outside \(\operatorname{spec}(H_f)\),
\[
(E-H_f)^{-1}
\]
is self-adjoint, hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f(E)^\dagger=\mathcal Y_f(E).
}
\label{eq:K1C_Hermitian}
\end{equation}

Therefore the reduced family problem is a finite Hermitian matrix problem on \(\mathcal H_L\), exactly as required for later K2, K3, and K4. 

\section{6. Practical K1C theorem}

We now formulate the practical theorem-level closure of K1C.

\begin{theorem}[Practical K1C theorem]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item K1B holds, i.e. the reference three-defect operator has an isolated rank-three low spectral subspace \(\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)\);
\item the heavy spectrum is separated by a gap \(\Delta_H>0\) as in \eqref{eq:K1C_gap};
\item each sector perturbation \(\delta\mathbb O_f\) is sufficiently small compared with \(\Delta_H\), and the low-heavy mixing block \(K_f\) is perturbative.
\end{enumerate}
Then for each sector \(f\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L
\text{ is preserved effectively,}
}
\end{equation}
in the precise sense that the exact low-energy dynamics near \(E_*\) is captured by the finite Hermitian operator
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f(E)
=
L_f
+
K_f(E-H_f)^{-1}K_f^\dagger
}
\label{eq:K1C_exact_again}
\end{equation}
or, to second order,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
-
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,Q_H
\bigl(Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q_H\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
+
O(\delta\mathbb O_f^3).
}
\label{eq:K1C_second_order_again}
\end{equation}
Hence K1C is closed at practical theorem level.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Because of the heavy gap and perturbative low-heavy mixing, the heavy resolvent exists and obeys the norm bound \eqref{eq:K1C_resolvent_bound}. Therefore the Schur complement \eqref{eq:K1C_exact_feshbach} is well-defined and bounded on the finite-dimensional low space \(\mathcal H_L\). Self-adjointness of \(\mathbb O_f\) implies Hermiticity of \(\mathcal Y_f(E)\). Thus the low sector is preserved effectively and the full sectoral problem reduces to a finite Hermitian operator on the same common family space.
\end{proof}

\section{7. Honest status}

This closes K1C at practical theorem level, but conditionally on K1B and on the survival of the heavy gap under sector perturbations.

So the exact honest conclusion is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1C\text{ is no longer conceptually open.}
}
\end{equation}
What remains is explicit microscopic evaluation of
\[
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L,\qquad
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,Q_H,\qquad
Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H,
\]
for the actual TECT defect PDE.

\section{Final Summary}

K1C asks whether sector perturbations preserve the common family space effectively.

Assuming K1B has already isolated a rank-three low spectral subspace
\[
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L),
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_L=3,
\]
with heavy complement \(Q_H=1-P_L\) separated by a gap \(\Delta_H\), the sector-dependent microscopic operator
\[
\mathbb O_f=\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}+\delta\mathbb O_f
\]
has the exact low-energy effective operator
\[
\mathcal Y_f(E)
=
L_f
+
K_f(E-H_f)^{-1}K_f^\dagger
\]
on \(\mathcal H_L\).

Expanding around the unperturbed low energy \(E_*\), this becomes
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
-
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,Q_H
\bigl(Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q_H\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
+
O(\delta\mathbb O_f^3).
}
\]

Thus effective preservation means not literal invariance of \(\mathcal H_L\), but the existence of a closed finite Hermitian low-energy operator on the same three-dimensional family space.

If the heavy gap survives and the low-heavy mixing is perturbative, then \(\mathcal Y_f\) is well-defined and Hermitian.
Therefore K1C is closed at practical theorem level.

So once K1B holds, the flavor problem is rigorously reduced to a finite Hermitian matrix problem on the common family space.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{K1D: Physical Left-Handed Interpretation of the Common Family Space}

\section{1. Input from K1A--K1C}

Assume K1A--K1C have already been established at practical theorem level.

Then:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L),
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_L=3,
}
\label{eq:K1D_HL}
\end{equation}
is the isolated common family subspace, and for each sector \(f\) the low-energy dynamics is captured by a finite Hermitian operator on \(\mathcal H_L\).

However, this does not yet imply that \(\mathcal H_L\) is the \emph{physical left-handed} family space.
K1D asks for precisely this additional interpretation.

\section{2. Mirror lifting as the first left-handedness requirement}

Let the defect zero-mode space decompose as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Z_L = Z_{\mathrm{top}}\oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
Z_R = Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^R,
}
\label{eq:K1D_zero_split}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\dim Z_{\mathrm{top}}=I,
\qquad
\dim Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L=\dim Z_R=:r.
\]

Let the effective chiral-odd mirror-lifting operator on the zero-mode sector be
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix},
}
\label{eq:K1D_Mmirror}
\end{equation}
relative to the splitting \(Z_L=Z_{\mathrm{top}}\oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\), \(Z_R\).

If
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mu:Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\to Z_R
\text{ is invertible,}
}
\label{eq:K1D_muinv}
\end{equation}
then all vectorlike mirror pairs are lifted to nonzero energy and only the index-protected chiral sector remains massless:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{massless sector} = Z_{\mathrm{top}}\subset Z_L,
\qquad
Z_R \text{ carries no massless mirror zero modes.}
}
\label{eq:K1D_top_only}
\end{equation}
This is already an exact finite-dimensional theorem. 

So after mirror lifting, the right-handed obstruction is reduced to zero at the massless level.

\section{3. Weak-bundle inclusion test}

Now let \(\mathcal E_W\) be the candidate rank-two weak bundle, with local orthonormal frame
\[
\Psi_1,\Psi_2.
\]
Let
\[
P_L:\mathcal E_0\to Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
P_R:\mathcal E_0\to Z_R
\]
denote the orthogonal projectors onto the protected left-handed sector and the right-handed sector.

Define the two Gram matrices
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(L)}_{AB}
=
\langle \Psi_A,P_L\Psi_B\rangle,
\qquad
G^{(R)}_{AB}
=
\langle \Psi_A,P_R\Psi_B\rangle,
\qquad
A,B=1,2.
}
\label{eq:K1D_Grams}
\end{equation}

Then the exact weak-chirality criterion is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0.
}
\label{eq:K1D_Gram_test}
\end{equation}

Indeed:
\[
G^{(R)}=0
\iff
P_R\Psi_A=0\ \text{ for }A=1,2,
\]
so the weak bundle has no right-handed component, while
\[
\det G^{(L)}\neq0
\iff
P_L\Psi_1,\ P_L\Psi_2
\text{ are linearly independent,}
\]
so the full rank-two weak bundle survives inside the protected left-handed sector.

Hence \eqref{eq:K1D_Gram_test} is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal E_W\subset Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
\mathcal E_W\cap Z_R=0.
}
\label{eq:K1D_bundle_inclusion}
\end{equation}
This is exactly the physical \(SU(2)_L\) chirality-selection criterion. 

\section{4. Reduction to a single complex overlap under multiplicity-one}

Assume now the multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal E_W\) is a multiplicity-one \(2\)-dimensional isotypic component of the low-energy bundle;
\item the protected left-handed target sector inside \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\) carrying the weak doublet is also a multiplicity-one copy of the same \(2\)-dimensional irrep.
\end{enumerate}

Choose an orthonormal basis \(u_1,u_2\) of that protected left-handed target doublet, and define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{mA}:=\langle u_m,\Psi_A\rangle.
}
\label{eq:K1D_U}
\end{equation}

Then \(U:\mathcal E_W\to Z_{\mathrm{top}}^{(2)}\) is an intertwiner between two copies of the same irreducible \(2\)-dimensional module.
By Schur's lemma,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2
}
\label{eq:K1D_sI}
\end{equation}
for some complex number \(s\).

Since
\[
G^{(L)}=U^\dagger U,
\]
one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det G^{(L)}=|\det U|^2=|s|^4.
}
\label{eq:K1D_det_s}
\end{equation}

Therefore the full-rank left-handed weak-bundle test reduces to the single overlap condition
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
s\neq0,
\qquad
s=\langle u_1,\Psi_1\rangle
\ \text{ in a symmetry-adapted basis.}
}
\label{eq:K1D_single_overlap}
\end{equation}
:contentReference[oaicite:8]{index=8}

\section{5. Physical left-handed family interpretation theorem}

We can now formulate the K1D theorem in the sharpest honest form.

\begin{theorem}[Practical K1D theorem]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item K1A--K1C hold at practical theorem level, so that \(\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)\) is the isolated common three-dimensional family space;
\item the mirror-lifting block \(\mu:Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\to Z_R\) is invertible;
\item a candidate weak rank-two bundle \(\mathcal E_W\) exists;
\item the weak-bundle projector test satisfies
\[
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
Then the surviving common family space is physically left-handed:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L
\text{ is the physical left-handed family space.}
}
\label{eq:K1D_conclusion}
\end{equation}
More precisely, the weak doublet bundle is contained entirely in the protected left-handed sector and has no right-handed massless partner.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By invertibility of \(\mu\), all vectorlike mirror pairs are lifted and only the index-protected chiral sector \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\subset Z_L\) remains massless. Thus there is no surviving massless right-handed mirror obstruction.

Now apply the projector test. The condition \(G^{(R)}=0\) implies the candidate weak bundle has no right-handed component. The condition \(\det G^{(L)}\neq0\) implies the projected weak bundle has full rank inside the protected left-handed sector. Hence the weak bundle is entirely left-handed in the surviving massless spectrum.

Therefore the isolated common family space obtained in K1A--K1C is not merely a low-energy three-dimensional space: it is the physical left-handed family space.
\end{proof}

\section{6. Strongest practical reduction of K1D}

Under the multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis, K1D reduces even further:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mu \text{ invertible}
\quad+\quad
G^{(R)}=0
\quad+\quad
s\neq0
\Longrightarrow
K1D.
}
\label{eq:K1D_final_reduction}
\end{equation}

So the genuinely remaining microscopic content of K1D is finite:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{compute the mirror pairing block }\mu,
\quad
\text{compute }G^{(R)},
\quad
\text{compute one complex overlap }s.
}
\label{eq:K1D_finite_tasks}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Honest status}

This closes K1D at practical theorem level, but conditionally.

The reason is exactly the same as before:
\begin{enumerate}
\item mirror lifting is exact if \(\mu\) is invertible, but full microscopic TECT still has to produce such an invertible \(\mu\);
\item the weak-bundle projector test is exact, but the actual defect PDE still has to provide the concrete overlaps.
\end{enumerate}

So the strongest honest conclusion is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1D \text{ is no longer conceptually open.}
}
\end{equation}
It is reduced to the finite microscopic evaluation of
\[
\mu,\qquad G^{(R)},\qquad s.
\]

\section{Final Summary}

K1D asks whether the common isolated family space \(\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)\) is the physical left-handed family space rather than just an arbitrary low-energy subspace.

This is reduced to two exact finite tests.

First, mirror lifting:
\[
Z_L=Z_{\mathrm{top}}\oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
Z_R=Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^R.
\]
If the mirror pairing block
\[
\mu:Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\to Z_R
\]
is invertible, then all vectorlike mirror pairs are lifted and only the protected chiral sector
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}\subset Z_L
\]
remains massless.

Second, weak-bundle chirality selection:
for a candidate weak frame \(\Psi_1,\Psi_2\), define
\[
G^{(L)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_L\Psi_B\rangle,
\qquad
G^{(R)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_R\Psi_B\rangle.
\]
Then
\[
\boxed{
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0
}
\]
is equivalent to
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_W\subset Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
\mathcal E_W\cap Z_R=0.
}
\]

Therefore:
\[
\boxed{
\mu \text{ invertible}
\quad+\quad
G^{(R)}=0
\quad+\quad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0
\Longrightarrow
\mathcal H_L \text{ is the physical left-handed family space.}
}
\]

Under the multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis, the overlap map is
\[
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2,
\]
so
\[
\det G^{(L)}=|s|^4,
\]
and K1D reduces further to the finite test
\[
\boxed{
\mu \text{ invertible},
\qquad
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
s\neq0.
}
\]

Thus K1D is closed at practical theorem level.
What remains is only the finite microscopic evaluation of the mirror block and the weak-bundle overlap data.

\section{Full Summary of \(K1\): Common Left-Handed Family Subspace}

\section{1. Position of \(K1\) inside the C4 program}

Inside the C4 matter/family sector, the internal tree is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1:\ \text{common left-handed family subspace},
\qquad
K2:\ \text{canonical kinetic normalization},
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K3:\ \text{nondegenerate splittings},
\qquad
K4:\ \text{hierarchy seed},
\qquad
K5:\ \text{irreducible CP seed}.
}
\end{equation}
The logical order is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1 \to K2 \to (K3,K4) \to K5.
}
\end{equation}

Thus \(K1\) is the first indispensable CKM precondition:
before one can normalize, split, or mix families, one must first construct a common left-handed family space.

\section{2. Internal decomposition of \(K1\)}

The common-family-space condition decomposes as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1A \to K1B \to K1C \to K1D,
}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1A:\ \text{common carrier sector exists},
\qquad
K1B:\ \text{a three-dimensional family subspace is isolated},
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1C:\ \text{sector perturbations preserve the family space effectively},
\qquad
K1D:\ \text{physical left-handed interpretation}.
}
\end{equation}

So \(K1\) is not one theorem but a chain:
\[
\text{single-defect protected mode}
\to
\text{three-defect rank-3 low sector}
\to
\text{sectorally stable family space}
\to
\text{physical left-handed identification}.
\]

\section{3. K1A: one defect \(\leftrightarrow\) one protected light mode}

\subsection{3.1. Conceptual target}

K1A asks whether one isolated defect supports exactly one protected light mode in the relevant low sector.

At the abstract level, this is already reduced to a Callias-type theorem:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Callias/Fredholm structure}
+
\text{asymptotic gap}
+
\text{topological nontriviality}
+
\operatorname{Ind}\bigl(\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}\bigr)=1
\Longrightarrow
\text{one exponentially localized protected mode.}
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{3.2. Working-branch reduction}

On the explicit working branch, the one-defect problem is already reduced to the finite acceptance data
\[
(g_c,g_E,g_O,\mu_\infty,\kappa_*).
\]
In the practically most relevant lowest-channel case \(|\kappa_*|=1\), the decisive inequalities reduce to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mu_\infty>0,
\qquad
\xi_E g_E>\xi_c g_c,
}
\end{equation}
together with competitor exclusion and remote-gap control.

The coefficient dictionary is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\frac{\lambda_c}{2}I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
\alpha=I_\theta\Lambda_E,
\qquad
\beta=I_\theta\Lambda_O,
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_c=\frac{\lambda_\parallel}{C_c},
\qquad
g_E=\frac{\alpha}{C_E},
\qquad
g_O=\frac{\beta}{C_O}.
}
\end{equation}

Longitudinally, the finite scalar reduction is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_c^{(JJ)}
\sim
\frac{\alpha_X^2}{M_X^2}\,
\mathcal N_{JJ}\,
m_\parallel\,
u_\parallel\,
|G_*|\,A_{G_*},
}
\end{equation}
while transversely
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Lambda_E
=
\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,\mathfrak T_E,
\qquad
\Lambda_O
=
\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,\mathfrak T_O.
}
\end{equation}

So the one-defect problem is no longer conceptual:
it is an explicit finite scalar audit on the intended Class II branch.

\subsection{3.3. Honest status of K1A}

K1A is closed at practical theorem level on the explicit canonical working branch.
It is not yet a full unconditional microscopic TECT theorem, because the actual TECT defect operator still has to verify the abstract assumptions and the final finite branch data still have to be inserted.

\section{4. K1B: spectral isolation of the three-family subspace}

\subsection{4.1. Conceptual target}

Assume one defect carries one localized protected light mode \(\psi_\star\).
For three sufficiently separated defects at positions \(X_a\), define translated approximate modes
\[
\psi_a(x)=\psi_\star(x-X_a),\qquad a=1,2,3.
\]
Because \(\psi_\star\) is exponentially localized,
\[
\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle
=
\delta_{ab}+O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}}).
\]
So the Gram matrix is
\[
G=I+O(e^{-\mu R_{\min}}),
\]
hence invertible for sufficiently large separation.

Therefore the approximate low subspace
\[
\mathcal S_{\rm app}=\operatorname{span}\{\psi_1,\psi_2,\psi_3\}
\]
is already three-dimensional.

\subsection{4.2. Exact light projector}

Let \(\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\) be the microscopic reference operator on the three-defect background.
If the heavy spectrum stays separated by a gap larger than the tunneling scale, then there exists a contour \(\Gamma\) enclosing exactly the three low eigenvalues.
Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P_L
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\oint_\Gamma
(z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}\,dz.
}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L:=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L),
\qquad
\operatorname{rank}P_L=3.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4.3. Honest status of K1B}

K1B is closed at practical theorem level, conditional on K1A and on sufficiently large defect separation / heavy-gap control.

\section{5. K1C: effective preservation under sector perturbations}

\subsection{5.1. Conceptual target}

K1C asks whether the isolated common family space \(\mathcal H_L\) survives sector perturbations effectively.

This does not mean strict invariance
\[
\delta\mathbb O_f\,\mathcal H_L\subset\mathcal H_L.
\]
The correct meaning is that the exact low-energy dynamics near the family cluster is reducible to a closed finite operator on \(\mathcal H_L\).

\subsection{5.2. Exact effective family operator}

Write
\[
\mathbb O_f
=
\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}
+
\delta\mathbb O_f,
\qquad
Q_H=1-P_L.
\]
Then the exact Feshbach/Schur-complement family operator is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f(E)
=
L_f
+
K_f(E-H_f)^{-1}K_f^\dagger,
}
\end{equation}
where
\[
L_f=P_L\mathbb O_fP_L,\qquad
K_f=P_L\mathbb O_fQ_H,\qquad
H_f=Q_H\mathbb O_fQ_H.
\]

To second order around the reference energy \(E_*\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
-
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,Q_H
\bigl(Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q_H\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
+
O(\delta\mathbb O_f^3).
}
\end{equation}

Hence sector perturbations preserve the family space effectively whenever the heavy gap survives and the low-heavy mixing is perturbative.

\subsection{5.3. Honest status of K1C}

K1C is closed at practical theorem level, conditional on K1B and on heavy-gap/mixing control.

\section{6. K1D: physical left-handed interpretation}

\subsection{6.1. Conceptual target}

Even after K1A--K1C, \(\mathcal H_L\) is still only a common low-energy family subspace.
K1D asks whether it is the \emph{physical left-handed} family space.

\subsection{6.2. Mirror lifting}

Decompose the zero-mode sector as
\[
Z_L=Z_{\mathrm{top}}\oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
Z_R=Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^R.
\]
If the mirror block
\[
\mu:Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\to Z_R
\]
has full rank, then all vectorlike mirror pairs are lifted and only
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}\subset Z_L
\]
remains massless.

Thus the right-handed massless obstruction is reduced to zero after mirror lifting.

\subsection{6.3. Weak-bundle projector test}

Let \(\mathcal E_W\) be the candidate weak rank-two bundle with local frame \(\Psi_1,\Psi_2\), and let \(P_L\), \(P_R\) project respectively onto the protected left-handed and right-handed sectors.
Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(L)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_L\Psi_B\rangle,
\qquad
G^{(R)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_R\Psi_B\rangle.
}
\end{equation}

Then the exact finite chirality-selection test is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0.
}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal E_W\subset Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
\mathcal E_W\cap Z_R=0.
\]

\subsection{6.4. Multiplicity-one reduction}

Under the multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis, the overlap map becomes
\[
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2,
\]
so
\[
\det G^{(L)}=|s|^4.
\]
Thus K1D reduces further to the finite data
\[
\mu,\qquad G^{(R)},\qquad s.
\]

\subsection{6.5. Honest status of K1D}

K1D is closed at practical theorem level, conditional on mirror lifting and on the microscopic computation of the projector overlaps.

\section{7. Integrated status of \(K1\)}

The strongest honest integrated statement is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1A,\ K1B,\ K1C,\ K1D
\text{ are all closed at practical theorem level, conditionally.}
}
\end{equation}

More explicitly:
\begin{enumerate}
\item K1A reduces one defect to one protected light mode by a finite acceptance audit;
\item K1B lifts this to an isolated rank-three light family subspace \(\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)\);
\item K1C shows that all relevant sector perturbations reduce to finite Hermitian operators on the same \(\mathcal H_L\);
\item K1D identifies \(\mathcal H_L\) as the physical left-handed family space via mirror lifting and weak-bundle projector tests.
\end{enumerate}

\section{8. What is still not unconditional}

K1 is not yet a final unconditional microscopic TECT theorem because several steps still require explicit finite microscopic insertion:
\begin{itemize}
\item actual verification of the Callias-type assumptions for the TECT defect operator,
\item actual evaluation of the one-defect acceptance data \((g_c,g_E,g_O,\mu_\infty,\kappa_*)\),
\item actual heavy-gap/mixing control for the three-defect reference operator,
\item actual evaluation of the mirror block \(\mu\),
\item actual evaluation of the weak-bundle projector overlaps \(G^{(L)},G^{(R)}\), or equivalently the overlap scalar \(s\).
\end{itemize}

So the final honest conclusion is:

\textbf{
K1 is structurally complete and practically closed, but still awaits finite microscopic insertion to become a full first-principles TECT theorem.
}


\section{Final Summary}

The \(K1\) program is the construction of the common physical left-handed family space.

It decomposes as
\[
\boxed{
K1A \to K1B \to K1C \to K1D.
}
\]

\paragraph{K1A.}
One defect carries exactly one protected light mode.
On the explicit working branch, this has already been reduced to the finite one-defect acceptance audit
\[
(g_c,g_E,g_O,\mu_\infty,\kappa_*),
\]
and in the lowest-channel case to the sharp inequalities
\[
\mu_\infty>0,
\qquad
\xi_E g_E>\xi_c g_c,
\]
together with competitor exclusion and remote-gap control.

\paragraph{K1B.}
Three sufficiently separated defects then generate an isolated rank-three low spectral cluster.
The exact family space is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L),
\qquad
P_L=\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma (z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}dz,
\qquad
\operatorname{rank}P_L=3.
}
\]

\paragraph{K1C.}
Sector perturbations preserve this family space effectively, in the sense that the exact low-energy dynamics is captured by the finite Hermitian Schur-complement operator
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
-
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,Q_H
\bigl(Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q_H\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
+
O(\delta\mathbb O_f^3).
}
\]

\paragraph{K1D.}
The common family space is physical left-handed if mirror lifting removes the vectorlike sector and the weak-bundle projector test satisfies
\[
\boxed{
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0.
}
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal E_W\subset Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
\mathcal E_W\cap Z_R=0.
\]
Under the multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis this reduces further to the finite data
\[
\mu,\qquad G^{(R)},\qquad s.
\]

Therefore the strongest honest global status is
\[
\boxed{
K1A,\ K1B,\ K1C,\ K1D
\text{ are all closed at practical theorem level, conditionally.}
}
\]

K1 is thus structurally complete, but still not a full unconditional microscopic TECT theorem because the remaining content is finite microscopic insertion:
the actual defect-operator assumptions, the explicit one-defect audit data, the heavy-gap control, the mirror block \(\mu\), and the weak-bundle overlaps.

\section{K1 Full Summary}

\subsection{K1: Common Left-Handed Family Subspace}

\(K1\) asks for the construction and identification of the common physical left-handed family space.

It decomposes as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K1A \to K1B \to K1C \to K1D.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{K1A: One defect \texorpdfstring{$\leftrightarrow$}{<->} one protected light mode}

The single-defect problem was reduced to the one-defect acceptance data
\[
(g_c,g_E,g_O,\mu_\infty,\kappa_*).
\]

At the radial/operator level, the single-defect defect operator was reduced to the explicit radial first-order system with real dynamical coefficients
\[
a(r),\quad b(r),\quad c(r),\quad \mu(r),
\]
and the graded Callias core
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_{\kappa,\pm}^{\rm core}
=
\pm a(r)\frac{d}{dr}
+
W_\kappa^{\rm core}(r),
\qquad
W_\kappa^{\rm core}(r)=\mu(r)+\frac{\kappa b(r)}{r}.
}
\end{equation}

The \(g_O\)-term contributes only a purely imaginary phase shift and does not affect \(L^2\)-normalizability.

The protected-channel theorem was reduced to:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mu_\infty>0,\quad a_0>0,\quad b_0>0,\quad
\kappa_*=-1,\quad \kappa_{\rm comp}\ge 1
\Longrightarrow
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb H_{\rm C})=1
}
\end{equation}
under the lowest-channel practical assumptions and competing-channel exclusion.

In the explicit canonical working branch
\[
T_\star=T^1,\qquad \gamma_E=1,\qquad \gamma_O=0,
\]
the practical criterion reduced to the finite scalar checks
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
-1<R_c<1,
\qquad
\alpha>0,
\qquad
\mu_\infty>0,
\qquad
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{full}}>\eta_{\mathrm{rem},\rho},
}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_c=
\frac{\xi_c}{\xi_E}\frac{3}{32}\alpha_X(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\end{equation}

A more primitive longitudinal witness was also extracted:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,
\Gamma_{JJ}\,
m_\parallel\,u_\parallel(T^1)\,
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\beta_X\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta=0.
}
\end{equation}

So the strongest practical \(K1A\) statement is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Gamma_{JJ}\neq0,\ 
\beta_X\neq0,\ 
m_\parallel\neq0,\ 
u_\parallel(T^1)\neq0,\ 
\theta_0\kappa\neq0,\ 
\mu_\infty>0,\ 
-1<R_c<1,\ 
\Delta_{\rm rem}>0
}
\end{equation}
\[
\Longrightarrow
\]
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{one defect } \leftrightarrow \text{ one protected light mode.}
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{K1B: Rank-3 family subspace isolation}

Assuming \(K1A\), let \(\psi_\star\) denote the exponentially localized single-defect protected mode, and define
\[
\psi_a(x)=\psi_\star(x-X_a),\qquad a=1,2,3,
\]
for three sufficiently separated defects.

Then
\[
\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle
=
\delta_{ab}+O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}}),
\]
so the Gram matrix is invertible and the approximate low subspace
\[
\mathcal S_{\rm app}=\operatorname{span}\{\psi_1,\psi_2,\psi_3\}
\]
is three-dimensional.

If the heavy spectrum stays separated by a surviving gap \(\Delta_H\), then the exact Riesz projector
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P_L=\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma (z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}dz
}
\end{equation}
is well-defined and satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\operatorname{rank}P_L=3.
}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L:=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L),
\qquad
\dim\mathcal H_L=3.
}
\end{equation}

This is the isolated common family subspace.

\subsection{K1C: Effective preservation under sector perturbations}

For each sector \(f\), define
\[
\mathbb O_f=\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}+\delta\mathbb O_f,
\qquad
Q_H=1-P_L.
\]
Then the exact Feshbach/Schur-complement family operator on \(\mathcal H_L\) is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f(E)
=
L_f+K_f(E-H_f)^{-1}K_f^\dagger,
}
\end{equation}
where
\[
L_f=P_L\mathbb O_fP_L,\qquad
K_f=P_L\mathbb O_fQ_H,\qquad
H_f=Q_H\mathbb O_fQ_H.
\]

To second order,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
-
P_L\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,Q_H
\bigl(Q_H\mathbb O^{(0)}_{\rm 3def}Q_H-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q_H\,\delta\mathbb O_f\,P_L
+
O(\delta\mathbb O_f^3).
}
\end{equation}

Thus effective preservation does not mean literal invariance of \(\mathcal H_L\).
It means that the full low-energy sector remains exactly reducible to a finite Hermitian operator on the same \(\mathcal H_L\), provided the heavy gap survives and the low-heavy mixing is perturbative.

\subsection{K1D: Physical left-handed interpretation}

Even after K1A--K1C, \(\mathcal H_L\) is initially only a common low-energy family space.
To interpret it as the \emph{physical left-handed} family space, two additional tests are required.

\paragraph{Mirror lifting.}
Decompose the massless candidate sector as
\[
Z_L=Z_{\mathrm{top}}\oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
Z_R=Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^R.
\]
If the mirror block
\[
\mu:Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\to Z_R
\]
is invertible, all vectorlike mirror pairs are lifted, leaving only the protected chiral sector \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\subset Z_L\).

\paragraph{Weak-bundle projector test.}
For a candidate weak frame \(\Psi_1,\Psi_2\), define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(L)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_L\Psi_B\rangle,
\qquad
G^{(R)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_R\Psi_B\rangle.
}
\end{equation}
Then the exact chirality-selection test is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0.
}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal E_W\subset Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
\mathcal E_W\cap Z_R=0.
\]

Under the multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis, the overlap map reduces to
\[
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}=|s|^4,
\]
so \(K1D\) reduces further to the finite data
\[
\mu,\qquad G^{(R)},\qquad s.
\]
